0553573411|4.0|This book is the second installment of The Farseer trilogy, which begins with [[ASIN:055357339X Assassin's Apprentice (The Farseer Trilogy, Book 1)]]. This review does not contain (significant) spoilers for Royal Assassin, although the events of Assassin's Apprentice are discussed here.<br /><br />The Good:<br /><br />My reaction to Assassin's Apprentice was confusion. I wasn't quite sure what I read, because it was a fairly erratic. Perhaps intentionally, the tone of the book shifted around many times. This was perhaps because we were seeing the world through the eyes of a young character, and his perceptions change drastically as time passes.<br /><br />Luckily, Royal Assassin is more stable in tone. Hobb also breaks off her tumultuous love affair with her thesaurus, much to the improvement of the book. While Assassin's Apprentice had a nonstop barrage of extremely obscure words used, apparently, just for the sake of having obscure words, Royal Assassin does not suffer from this problem.<br /><br />Likewise, Assassin's Apprentice had issues finding its plot. Really, it served as a prologue for the series and little more. Royal Assassin focuses in more detail on what seems to be the true heart of the story: the Red-Ships and the power struggle between Prince Verity and Prince Regal. To be frank, more things happen in this book, and that is a good thing.<br /><br />The strong points of Assassin's Apprentice are kept in this book. The magic system is still quite interesting, and in this book we hear more about the Wit and less about the Skill. The world is still compelling, and the heightened focus on the Red-Ship plot line makes it even more gripping. The characters are still well fleshed out and compelling.<br /><br />The Neutral:<br /><br />The first third to half of Royal Assassin seems like it is just a meditation on honor, sacrifice, and duty. Almost every single important character in the book gives something major up for the sake of a greater good. Literallythe only main character who does not do this is the primary antagonist of the book.<br /><br />Other reviews have talked about how the main character in this book is powerless, cowardly or indecisive. I disagree with that interpretation. Instead, I feel like that is an element of the theme of sacrifice in this book: Fitz sacrifices his autonomy in service to his king. He certainly could use his assassin training to go on a poisoning spree and stand up for himself, but to do so would be a betrayal of everything he swore in the first book. Instead, he simply accepts that he is honor-bound to live his life however he is commanded, regardless of how he feels about the commands.<br /><br />If that sounds like a horrible protagonist to you, then you're going to have a bad time reading this book. However, once I realized what was happening, I appreciated the inherent conflict it presented.<br /><br />The Bad:<br /><br />There are conflicts between Royal Assassin and Assassin's Apprentice. I'm not sure if I would call them continuity errors, but there were several points in Royal Assassin that seemed to slightly retcon the previous book. Overall, I was left with the impression that the last chapter of Apprentice was added at the insistence of the editor or publisher to give it a more satisfying ending, and Royal just ignores everything that happens in that chapter.<br /><br />The ending, which I will not spoil, is anti-climactic to say the least. I won't discuss this in detail because it would require spoiling things, but suffice to say that the end of this book would give Neal Stephenson's early work a run for its money in terms of stories that just randomly stop. It's the middle book in a trilogy, so that is to be expected to some extent. It just seems like in this book, the ONLY significant events that happen at the end are to set up the plot of the next book.<br /><br />Finally, and this is a matter of personal preference, I did not find the romance subplot of this bookenjoyable. Again, I don't want to spoil anything, just suffice it to say that I found it more annoying than anything.<br /><br />Overall:<br /><br />Royal Assassin is, to me, a vast improvement over the first book of the series. It has all of the charm that kept me reading the first book, and did away with many of the things I found annoying. It has flaws of its own, but it's still a solid step up.<br /><br />Like the first book, it was compelling enough that I continually forced myself to make time for reading more of it. At the end of the day, that is more telling than any analysis I could perform about the merits of the writing or the story. I would recommend this to anyone who isn't completely put off by my caveats and complaints in this review. Hobb's work is definitely not for everyone, but I found it enjoyable.

How can I say. What I do know is that Rothfuss is an exceptional story teller, and unlike Martain, Rothfuss doesn't attempt a story on multiple fronts. Maybe this is why, if I need to compare, I prefer Rothfuss. A single setting in first person is an intense undertaking.<br /><br />Partick Rothfuss' Kvothe is one of the most compelling characters I've met in years. He is brilliant, but when we meet him, he is a child, and as a child he makes profound mistakes. We agonize for young Kvothe as we watch him struggle, sometimes for his very life. He is innocent, as children are, and in that way he is take advantage of. But young Kvothe grows, and with age comes wisdom, if we have the wherewithal to heed caution. Unfortunately, Kvothe is often reckless as teenagers are. Again, part of our love for him comes from his imperfection. He is human, and Rothfuss never let's us forget it.<br /><br />In addition to a hero worth adoring, Rothfuss has built a story that bends and sways. It is an unpredictable story, which makes it all the more real. Characters act the way that real people behave, which is vital in fantasy, as the world in which the characters inhabit is only slightly familiar to the reader.<br /><br />The language alone is reason enough to read this book. Line upon line will lift any poet or story teller's heart. You know you're in for a treat when the prologue's title is Silence in Three Parts.<br /><br />If you love fantasy, I highly suggest you pick this one up.<br />flag

0451457994|5.0|2001, in both film and book form, has had a profound impact on our culture. It has helped shape our relationship with technology, our search for other life forms, and our views on our evolution.<br /><br />The story of where we came from dovetails nicely here with the story of where we're going. In between is the story of HAL, both a cautionary tale about our reliance on technology and an exploration into what it means to be human.<br /><br />The story of humanity's origin gets a thoughtful treatment here as the notion that maybe homo sapien, a pathetic species which is poorly equipped for survival, got some outside help to not only survive, but thrive. That thread comes full circle at the end when the beings who provided that assistance again provide a boost to launch mankind to a new level of existence, one unhindered by the limitations of a mortal coil.<br /><br />As compelling and influential as these plot lines are, it's HAL's story that has served as a touchstone of our culture. His designers strive to make him human and that means building in frailty. Does that mean he thinks? Does he feel? He is capable of experiencing guilt, insecurity, and fear as part of the effort to match the human ideal. But it is these very characteristics that lead to tragic consequences. This poses important questions about what kind of reliance we should have on technology. It serves us, but what is the risk of that reliance?<br /><br />Since the movie is so widely known, someone considering reading this probably wants to know if the book fills in any gaps in the visually oriented but enigmatic film. The answer is \\"yes\\". The narrative voice and the inner voices of the characters fill in much that is left unexplained in the movie. Even the star gate sequence at the end is relatively clearly explained.<br /><br />The book was written at the same time as the screenplay, but differs on some points, usually for technical reasons. Kubrick was unable to create a convincing model of Saturn, so the voyage in the movie goes to Jupiter instead of Saturn, as it does in the book. The monolith at the beginning is clear in the book, but that proved difficult to film, so Kubrick made it black. Other differences are harder to explain. For example, in the book Floyd is a single father.<br /><br />2001, by almost any measure has had an enormous influence on our culture. The film pushed back the boundaries of what we can expect from visual entertainment. But the story is no less influential, providing an important backdrop to the driving force behind our civilization for the past forty years. The technology that fuels our culture and our relationship with it has been profoundly influenced by 2001. As such, it is important to understand 2001 to understand our modern world.

B000MRA5NS|5.0|I have to admit up front that I have a strong bias about the way I think James Bond should be portrayed. I like the old Ian Fleming style of Bond who is true to the books. We are talking a very serious Bond who is macho, resourceful and more than a little bit vengeful when he feels like he has been wronged. At the same time, the classic Bond of the Ian Fleming novels that I look for also sets the ladies on fire and is soo unafraid of his enemies that he literally laughs at them even as they appear to have his life in their hands. This guy never loses his cool and is the epitome of the word. He used to go by Sean Connery when off-camera but I feel very comfortable now saying that he goes by Daniel Craig these days.<br /><br />  In Casino Royale, we have a nice, modernized take on the very first of Flemings' Bond adventures. To summarize without giving away more than just basic plot devices for those who have not yet watched this movie, Bond is on a mission to smoke out a powerful money launderer who takes money and invests it for terrorist groups and other criminal organizations. Basically he demonstrates classic Bond wit by tricking his rival into losing all of his money in a game of chance which makes him a marked man by all the organizations he is a front for, rendering him an easy target for capture and use as an informant. Bond succeeds and in the process he manages to get into quite a few fights on the side. Despite being injured, again in classic-Bond style, he manages to get back to the table in no time flat and even clean up as if he never left his martini. The ladies fall for him and Bond himself, breaking one of his own rules to never fall for a lady, falls hard for his assistant, fellow MI6 agent Vesper Lynd. When she turns out to be a double-agent (not by her own choice), Bond goes on a rampage and is left scarred in the end. I should say more scarred as Bond is always a man who has a chip on his shoulder.<br /><br />  Craig hits the ground running in this his first journey into the BondVerse. He pulls off the character with a nice showing of suave but also with a serious dedication to dark justice right out of the novels, a cross between Connery's smooth demeanor and the more violent Bond of the Timothy Dalton years. The atmosphere, the music, the story and the acting is amazing and all seem to fall into place right where they belong. This is instantly one of my favorite Bond adventures.<br /><br />  The video and sound qualities are quite good, this being a recent hit release. I found no trouble here whatsoever- everything was crystal clear. I have read some reviews elsewhere of people having trouble watching this movie and others on certain players but on the PS3 it was just like at the theater as far as I was concerned.<br /><br />Conclusion<br /><br />  In short, buy this movie. If you like James Bond at all you will enjoy it. If you are like me and enjoy the more serious Bond of Connery and Dalton over the somewhat more comedic and slap-stick Roger Moore years, you will absolutely love it. I never knock anyone who played James Bond and for good reason- Double-O-7's shoes are very hard to fill. In fact, Will Smith's character is essentially alone (well, along with his German Shepherd) in a post-apocalyptic NYC wiped out by a mutating virus.  You might think this would make for a boring movie.  Some have found this movie to be that, but I did not.  The silent, self-reflective stretches of this movie give Dr. Neville (Will Smith's character) time to think, and we have time for that as well.  This makes for a richer experience, and makes me wonder if I've missed important thoughts or details in more action-packed movies, as I was carried along willy-nilly by the plot.  Obviously Will Smith's acting makes or breaks this movie; many could not carry a movie single-handedly the way this one requires.<br /><br />What I liked:  Will Smith strikes just the right balance between courage in the face of danger, and self-deprecating humor.  You would have to be comfortable in your own skin if you were, literally, the last man on earth.  The CG images of a NYC destroyed and overgrown by decay were mesmerizing and realistic.  I also liked the many small details of what he would have needed to do to survive.  Yes, fight the zombies; but you've also got to eat, and take care of your stuff.  Suspense of battling the inhuman night-creatures was riveting.  Some did not like the ending (no spoilers) but I was fine with it.<br /><br />What I didn't like:  In some ways not faithful to the book; but this was not a huge issue for me.  Others have felt that the plot got bogged down or derailed at times.  And extroverts would be disconcerted, and perhaps even bored, by the solitude of it all.  Most of the main character's witty dialog was with himself or his dog; not everone's kind of sci-fi movie.<br /><br />It's true that it was not what many of us have been conditioned to expect from the Hollywood movie assembly line.  Maybe that's why I liked it.

B00006GO99|5.0|The seven-song Unique Thelonious Monk (1956) platter was the pianist's second during his remarkable five-year tenure on Riverside. His debut for the label was the aptly titled Plays Duke Ellington (1955) and once again, on this disc, Monk's song selection did not feature any original compositions. Rather, the well-chosen standards included exemplify and help further establish the pianist and bandleader within the context of familiar melodies at the head of a trio -- consisting of Oscar Pettiford (bass) and Art Blakey (drums). Regarding the personnel, while Pettiford had also accompanied Monk on the Ellington sides, Blakey replaces Kenny Clarke. The pairing of Monk and Blakey cannot be overstated. Immediately, evidence of their uncanny instrumental interaction is the rhythmic focal point of \\"Liza, All the Clouds'll Roll Away\\" as the two play musical cat-and-mouse. They cajole and wheedle atop Pettiford's undulating undercurrent as it sonically corals their skilled syncopation and otherwise inspired mile-a-minute interjections. This is starkly contrast to the haunting, lyrical piano solo on \\"Memories of You.\\" Monk infuses the piece with such profound ingenuity and integrity that his re-evaluation and innovative arrangement are singularly and undeniably his own. Fats Waller's \\"Honeysuckle Rose\\" reels with a frolicking and ever-so-slightly inebriated gate. It is likewise highlighted by Monk's dreamlike single-note runs up and down the keyboard and the stride piano-style chord progressions that preserves a fluidity within the tune. The advanced score maintains a guise of almost goofy abandon within Monk's highly logical and well-sculpted musical structure. The juxtaposition of \\"Darn That Dream\\" is another study in the vacillating moods of The Unique Thelonious Monk. The sophisticated performance is understated, yet remains loose and limber and perfectly in keeping with the album's leitmotif of exploring Monk's skills as an arranger and musician. As if he were testing his audience, the manic and atonal opening to \\"Tea for Two\\" -- briefly featuring Pettiford on bowed upright bass -- rollicks with a youthful visage, rather than being a simple reworking of this well-established classic. This LP concludes with one of Monk's most memorable pieces on the fun and freewheeling \\"Just You, Just Me.\\" The trio struts and glides as Monk's intricate fingering simultaneously displays his physical dexterity as well as his ability to play so deftly in the moment. Both attributes would resurface ten-fold once Monk began to animate his own compositions on the genre-defining Brilliant Corners (1956).audience, the manic and atonal opening to \\"Tea for Two\\" -- briefly featuring Pettiford on bowed upright bass -- rollicks with a youthful visage, rather than being a simple reworking of this well-established classic. Frank Bullitt (Steve McQueen) is asked by local politician Walter Chalmers (Robert Vaughn) to protect a witness named Johnny Ross who is scheduled to testify before a Senate subcommittee on organized crime. Bullitt assigns his trusted Sgt. Delgetty (Don Gordon) and another officer (Carl Reindel) to take shifts with Ross, who is holed up at a cheap hotel, but the mob's hitmen get to Ross anyway. Bullitt is determined to find the killers, but the deeper he digs into the case, the more it seems there is more to it than meets the eye. Meanwhile, Chalmers blames Bullitt for losing his witness and makes a nuisance of himself, trying to obstruct the investigation.<br /><br />Steve McQueen plays the laconic tough-guy anti-hero that he was so good at here. The actor, an avid racecar driver himself, must have appreciated the 10-minute car chase on the streets in and around San Francisco that earned \\"Bullitt\\" a place in the cinematic canon. In 2008, Ford released a dark green Mustang Bullitt in honor of the film. William A. Fraker's stylish, fluid camerawork is also memorable. For a film with a measured pace and introverted characters, it has a lot of energy, because the camera, itself, is often on the move. The jazz-inspired score by Lalo Schifrin adds to the 1960s cool. And Robert Duvall has a small role as a cabbie with a good memory. After 45 years, \\"Bullitt\\" still conveys a sense of cool detachment punctuated by high emotions and harsh realities.

If the answer is yes, then this film should be fine for them as well.  I really don't think there is any film that makes a better comparison with this one than Casablanca.  They're both film noir, they're both drenched in 1930s terminology, speech patterns, character dynamics and scenarios, and they both contain most of the same things that modern adults don't like kids seeing.  Guns, cigarettes, liquer, etc...  No foul language in either case, but that was expected in the 30s.  This movie isn't any tamer than Casablanca, despite being animated, but it's also no less tame, despite being made in the 90s.<br /><br />Still, Casablanca was one of the greatest classic films of all time, so the very fact that I'm comparing Porco Rosso with it repeatedly is probably a sign of something good. B001KVZ6HK|5.0|It's pretty clear that Marvel's entire film-making machine was leading up to this effort, a movie that nobody thought could be made because it involved Hollywood putting aside egos, directors joining forces, and intellectual property being leveraged in such a way that everyone plays nicely together behind the scenes, even if the characters don't play nicely together on screen.  Somehow, God bless them, Marvel actually pulled it off.<br /><br />Picking up where \\"Captain America,\\" \\"Thor,\\" and \\"Iron Man\\" left off, the Strategic Homeland, Intervention, Enforcement and Logistics Division (SHIELD) led by Nicky Fury (Samuel L. Jackson), commands a ragtag band of agents that just happen to work on a flying aircraft carrier: Maria Hill (Cobie Smulders, unfortunately miscast), Clint Barton (Jeremy Renner, not quite as sarcastic as he should be) as Hawkeye, and Natasha Romanoff (Scarlett Johnannson) as Black Widow. Their mission is to retrieve the Tesseract (AKA the Cosmic Cube) from Asgardian Loki (Tom Hiddleston) and his Predator-like race of alien soldiers known as the Chitauri.<br /><br />There are so many ways this film could have gone wrong, and part of \\"The Avengers\\"' success is that Marvel took its time building up to this moment with its previous releases. Asgardian god-hero Thor (Chris Hemsworth) could easily become a walking caricature, but he's such a tower presence that he demands to be taken seriously. Steve Rogers (Chris Evans) as Captain America is slightly diminished here, but he settles comfortably into his role as a brilliant tactician out of time but not out of his depth. All these serious characters are just banter-fodder for Tony Stark (Robert Downey Jr.) as Iron Man, who shamelessly stirs things up when things goes down.  The one worrisome character was Bruce Banner (Mark Ruffalo) as the Hulk. With two tepid films under the character's belt, the Hulk was potentially \\"The Avengers\\" weakest link.<br /><br />No need to worry.  The Hulk is one of the best parts of the film.  Ruffalo plays Banner as a scruffy Doctor Without Borders, a brilliant man who gives off a zen vibe because you wouldn't like him when he's angry. Joss Whedon, master director of all things geeky, knows how to build up anticipation.  Whether it's the massive firepower that's never brought to bear against Banner or the trembling lip of the deadly Black Widow, we're always reminded that at any moment \\"The Big Guy\\" might show up. When the Hulk finally does arrive, he does not disappoint; the Hulk's rage is exhilarating and terrifying to behold, a green King Kong. Also, the Hulk's got a great sense of humor, embodied by his rivalry with the only guy who can stand up to him, Thor.<br /><br />Whedon does an excellent job of balancing the weaker characters, notably Black Widow and Hawkeye, against the superbeings who stride amongst them. Black Widow has two scenes that demonstrate how deadly she really is even against god-like beings, and Hawkeye's archery is unparalleled. There are not one but two tracking shots in which every character struts their stuff.  If you go to \\"The Avengers\\" film to see them duke it out, against each other and alien monsters, you've come to the right place. This is going to be hard to top.<br /><br />And oh yeah...shawarma is a real food.  Stay to the end of the credits.rts of the film. B0080CL0HM|4.0|The quote used in the caption for this review is form the writer of the novel on which this film BEL AMI is based - Guy de Maupassant, whose stories are characterized by their economy of style and efficient, effortless d&eacute;nouement. In many ways that quote summarizes the plot of this story.<br /><br />BEL AMI  is set in Paris in the upper-middle class environment of the leading journalists of the newspaper La Vie Franaise and their friends. It tells the story of Georges Duroy (Robert Pattinson), who has spent three years of military service in Algeria. After six months working as a clerk in Paris, an encounter with his former comrade, Forestier (Philip Glenister), enables him to start a career as a journalist. From a reporter of minor events and soft news, he gradually climbs his way up to chief editor. Duroy initially owes his success to Forestier's wife (Uma Thurman) who helps him write his first articles and, when he later starts writing lead articles, she adds an edge and poignancy to them. At the same time, she uses her connections among leading politicians to provide him with behind the scenes information which allows him to become actively involved in politics. Duroy is also introduced to many politicians in Madame Forestier's drawing-room. Duroy becomes the lover of Forestiers' friend Clotilde Marelle (Christina Ricci), another influential woman. Duroy later tries to seduce Madeleine Forestier to get even with her husband, but she repulses Duroy's sexual advances and offers that they become true friends without ulterior motives instead. In a few months, Charles Forestier's health deteriorates and he travels to Cannes to regain it. Soon afterwards, Duroy receives a letter from Madeleine imploring him to come to join her and help her bear the last moments of her husband's life. As Forestier dies, Duroy asks Madeleine to marry him. After a few weeks to consider, she agrees. Georges now signs his articles Du Roy (an aristocratic style of French name) in order to add prestige to his name. The married couple travel to Normandy, the region of Georges's childhood, and meet his peasant parents. Finding the reality different than her romantic expectations, Madeleine feels very uncomfortable with his parents and so their stay with them is short. In the newspaper office, Duroy is ridiculed for having his articles written by his wife, just as the late Forestier had his articles written by her. His newspaper colleagues call him `Forestier', which drives Georges mad and he suddenly becomes heavily jealous of Madeleine, insisting that she admit having been unfaithful to Forestier, but she never does.<br /><br />In order to suppress the stings of jealousy, Duroy starts an affair with Mme Virginie Walter (Kristin Scott Thomas), the wife of the owner of the newspaper. He especially enjoys the conquest as he is her first extramarital lover. Later on, however, he regrets the decision, for he cannot get rid of her when he does not want her. Duroy's relations with his wife become chillier, and at one point he takes a police superintendent to a flat in which his wife is meeting a minister. They catch the two in the act of adultery, which was then a crime punishable under the law.<br /><br />Duroy's ascent to power continues. Duroy, now a single man, makes use of his chief Rousset (Colm Meaney) daughter's (Holliday Grainger) infatuation with him, and arranges an elopement with her. The parents then have no other choice but to grant their assent to the marriage. The last chapter shows Duroy savouring his success at the wedding ceremony at which 'all those who figured prominently in society' were present. His thoughts, however, chiefly belong to Mme de Marelle who, when wishing him all the best, indicates that she has forgiven him for his new marriage and that their intimate meetings can be taken up again.<br /><br />Directors Declan Donnellan and Nick Ormerod are novices and the pacing of the film could use some polish. The sets and costumes are the stars and the musical score by both Rachel Portman and Lakshmam Joseph De Saram and cinematography by Stefano Falivene mange to creat the atmosphere of La Belle Epoque well.  The cast is strong, though the title role of Georges Duroy (aka Bel Ami) feels undercast: Robert Pattinson lacks the depth of subtlety in his current acting skill to be convincing as the poor cad of this rags to riches via abuse of women role.  It will be interesting to see if this film will last once it is released in theaters. Grady Harp, May 12re the stars and the musical score by both Rachel Portman and Lakshmam Joseph De Saram and cinematography by Stefano Falivene mange to creat the atmosphere of La Belle Epoque well. It will be interesting to see if this film will last once it is released in theaters. Grady Harp, May 12

B00005J6T1|4.0|Released in 1983 and taking its' own sweet time to catch on, Madonna's self-titled debut album is still a fun collection of light, fluffy early '80s tunes including the hit singles \\"Holiday\\", \\"Lucky Star\\" and \\"Borderline\\" as well as some great lesser-known material inspired by her days at Danceteria.<br /><br />The infectious cowbell-and-drum-machine driven \\"Holiday\\" remains a dance-floor favorite nearly 30 years later and is known by heart even by non-fans, but the hypnotic \\"Everybody\\" surpasses it for sheer dance-floor intoxication. The bland \\"Borderline\\" never really grew on me--it sounded a little dated even then. The non-hits \\"Think of Me\\", \\"Physical Attraction\\" and \\"I Know It\\" feature fun, synth-heavy '80s arrangements and even in this early stage Madonna shows range. When she goes from pouting \\"Tell me what I did to you to make you treat me so badly\\" to scolding \\"You better, you better\\" in \\"Think of Me\\" or sexily and brazenly challenging her undecisive lover (\\"What are you gonna do?\\") in the delightfully futuristic, celestial \\"Physical Attraction\\" her delivery is perfect.<br /><br />The real showstopper here is the scorching ode to female horniness that is \\"Burning Up\\". In one of her most convincing performances Madonna proclaims her sexual frustration by first simply stating \\"I can't quench my desire\\" then in a final desperate confession \\"I have no shame\\". Nasty little guitar licks underscore Madonna's heavy panting in this lust-drenched dance-rocker. While the original album cut is the best thing about \\"Madonna\\", make sure you get the remastered CD with the Bonus Tracks because the 12\\" version actually improves on it. Though the guitars are missing, the extra frantic keyboards make up for it and Madonna's vocals are more in the forefront.<br /><br />Madonna's fate was already sealed with this album--she would quickly become and be forever known as the Queen of Dance Music. Still a fun ride all these years later. 0375413405|5.0|I first read reviews on French Amazon, where most reviewers appear to have come to this volume through the movie Julie and Julia, and seem to find it a minor contribution to their knowledge base.  I guess my age is showing, but I first heard Julia on TV in the early 1960s when I was a California teenager, and I had never in my life heard about cooking like this. I bought this book then (and I still have that same copy).  Actually I bought it originally to give to my mother, who was frankly a terrible cook who hated being in the kitchen, but she was insulted at the implication, so I kept it.  Julia introduced me, and America, to accessible French cuisine.  Before Julia, US cuisine was a wasteland, and those who first come to cooking today clearly don't realize how great an influence she was then.  Much has happened in the culinary world since Julia, and today her book perhaps does seem a bit dated...we already KNOW this and that...but then we didn't, and she was a revelation.  She changed my life. This is a brilliant book, despite all the butter.  There is no other like it.

Overall I agree that it is well written and generally held my interest, but I don't personally view it as a classic of sci-fi. Maybe it would have resonated with me far more if I had read it at the age of 12 or 13, rather than 25. While Ender does deal with some hard-edged stuff like murder, genocide, and family issues, much of the plot revolves around his anxieties about making friends, bullies, and dealing with school pressure. Worthwhile topics for sure, just not as relevant to some audiences as to others.<br /><br />Aside from that, my only major issue with the book is the side story of valentine and peter transforming into political demagogues. This is where the book drags, badly. I suppose it was necessary to include something to break up Ender's often repetitive scenes, but unfortunately these side vignettes add little of interest or value to the overall story. In more than one case I found myself skimming through those sections.<br /><br />In any case, the story is decent and the ending was satisfying; I just didn't find the book to be all that memorable.

B0073ODRKG|5.0|Simple put, this is an incredible amount of quality music at a fantastic price.  And, like the review title says, this is perfect if you've only had a taste of Madonna and would like to dig a little deeper than say \\"Celebration\\" or any of her other hits compilations or, like me, you're an all out Madonna geek and you try to snap up all of her output for your collection.  The five star rating for me is, again, based simply on this amazing body of musical work, but, no matter who you are, this packaging is somewhat disappointing.  The compact size of the case is an absolute plus and no doubt contributed to the low cost of the final product, but Warner Music could've absolutely put a hair more effort into this particularly given that M is an artist who has consistently put a lot of thought and design into her album art.  The clamshell case is a matte finish heavy card sleeve which can get damaged and scuffed a little too easily and the print quality on many of the card sleeves is a bit muddled looking.  Funnily enough Warner Music Europe's repressing of her first 5 studio albums on vinyl have the same substandard print quality on their sleeves so perhaps in their rush to capitalize on the release of \\"MDNA\\" a few corners got cut.  Personally, I would've preferred a more substantial outer packaging, and then perhaps glossy finish card sleeves, but, hey, that's just me.  As most other reviewers have mentioned, there is a lot of important Madonna music not included here, including \\"Vogue\\", \\"Crazy for You\\", \\"Into the Groove\\", \\"Beautiful Stranger\\", and the Oscar winning \\"You Must Love Me\\" from \\"Evita\\" just to name a few.  But considering the staggering number of hit songs that are here and given that we're in the digital age where these missing pieces can be downloaded in the blink of an eye these omissions aren't deal breakers.  So, A+ music, but C+ for the packaging, and all in all an excellent value.  As to the albums themselves:<br />1.  MADONNA (1983) still sounds relatively fresh all of these years later and includes such modern pop classics as \\"Holiday\\" and \\"Lucky Star\\" and her enduring dance anthem \\"Everybody\\" which launched her career in April of 1982.<br />2.  LIKE A VIRGIN (1984) has sold over ten million copies in the U.S. alone but remains my least favorite of her records.  Yes, the title track and \\"Material Girl\\" are trademark Madonna anthems, but, singles aside, the rest of the album is mediocre.<br />3.  TRUE BLUE (1986) represented an absolute shift in her music towards a more adult sound beginning with haunting lead single \\"Live to Tell\\", and continuing on with tracks like \\"Papa Don't Preach\\" and \\"Open Your Heart\\", plus the mid-tempo Latin flavored \\"La Isla Bonita\\".  On the negative end of things the arrangements and synths fairly scream mid-eighties which dates the material considerably, and when she indulges in almost cute-sy, pop throwback songs like the title track and the percolating \\"Jimmy Jimmy\\" things get a bit saccharine for my taste.  Bonus points for the shoulda-been-a-single track \\"Where's the Party\\".<br />4.  LIKE A PRAYER (1989) was an absolute pinnacle at the time, and remains a high watermark not just for Madonna but for pop music in general.  It spun off 5 U.S. singles and an additional sixth single released internationally.  Her collaboration with Prince is a bit odd but intriguing, and funnily enough she recycled a few of the lyrics to their collaboration for 2005's hit \\"Hung Up\\".<br />5.  EROTICA (1992) was consciously, over the top, in your face, and this turned off some fans and instigated a major backlash following the almost simultaneous releases of her book \\"Sex\\" and notorious film flop \\"Body of Evidence\\".  Still, there's some great music here that's not to be missed including the title track which samples Kool & the Gang's \\"Jungle Boogie\\", the dance anthem \\"Deeper and Deeper\\", the mid-tempo \\"Bad Girl\\" and the beautiful ballad \\"Rain\\".<br />6.  BEDTIME STORIES (1994)  marked a shift into a moreR&B, hip hop influenced groove including collaborations with Dallas Austin, Babyface, and Nellee Hooper, and in retrospect making a perfect bridge between the previous record and her next, \\"Ray of Light\\".  Lead single \\"Secret\\" went gold and the gorgeous ballad \\"Take a Bow\\" remains her biggest hit in this country spending 7 weeks at number 1 on the Hot 100.<br />7.  RAY OF LIGHT (1998) was another benchmark and worldwide critical and commercial hit that combined the introspective songwriting she achieved with longtime collaborator Patrick Leonard with cutting edge electronica provided by techno wizard William Orbit.  A truly superb album.<br />8.  MUSIC (2000) Do you like to boogie-woogie?  The title track remains one of the most influential sounds of the new millennium.  Here she collaborates with Frenchman Mirwais Ahmadzai to create a techno sound combined with an almost folksy, acoustic influence.  More bonus points for yet another shoulda-beeen-a-single cut, the stunning dance floor stomper \\"Impressive Instant\\".<br />9.  AMERICAN LIFE (2003) Like \\"Erotica\\", this one went too far for some fans, and, admittedly, it is one of her more challenging records.  Musically the sound is very similar to \\"Music\\" as she works again with Ahmadzai, but here the folk influence is even more strongly felt on both the melodies and lyrics.  She covers much of the same subject matter as \\"Ray of Light\\" but without those songs' polish and finesse.  Still, it's a highly underrated album with many good qualities, including the hardcore dance track \\"Nobody Knows Me\\", the beautiful \\"Nothing Fails\\" which plays like the musical love child of \\"Don't Tell Me\\" and \\"Like a Prayer\\", the mid-tempo \\"Intervention\\", and the simple, stripped back ballad \\"X-Static Process\\".  As for her Bond theme \\"Die Another Day\\", you probably already either love it or hate it.<br />10.  CONFESSIONS ON A DANCE FLOOR (2005)  Working with Stuart Price she creates an absolute masterpiece here.  Sampling Abba lead single\\"Hung Up\\" was a record breaker the world over and managed to go top ten in the States despite a lack of radio support, second single \\"Sorry\\" references The Jackson 5 hit \\"Can You Feel It\\", and \\"Future Lovers\\" is a kick butt dance floor filling homage to the sound of 1970s disco maestro Giorgio Moroder.<br />11.  HARD CANDY (2008)  She worked here with her highest profile collaborators since '94 with mixed results, although after the euphoric high of \\"Dance Floor\\" almost anything would've been something of a let down.  \\"4 Minutes\\" was big hit, but for many Madonna fans was not representative of the album as a whole as it made Madonna sound like a guest star on a Justin Timberlake/Timbaland collaboration rather than the other way around.  Happily her work with Pharrell Williams and the Neptunes are much more satisfying including lead cut \\"Candy Shop\\" and second single the fierce \\"Give It 2 Me\\".  Her \\"Devil Wouldn't Recognize You\\" cuts it musically much too close to comfort to Timberlake's own earlier hit \\"Cry Me a River\\" but works nevertheless, and album closer \\"Voices\\" remains haunting.

Alot of people were complaining about the set being a little too tight. While I don't think this personally, I can see where they're coming from. The cushion inside the headphones aren't enough to keep your ears from rubbing up the inside of the set, which can cause some discomfort. I've found myself pulling the cup from my left ear away a bit just to get it a little more comfortable.<br /><br />2. For me personally, the wire could be a bit longer. While I have no issues with it, I can see how I could have had some. While I don't have a specific measurement for the wire, I'll just say those sitting a bit farther away from their actual computer, and have their monitor and such set up somewhere else, you may find yourself needing a longer wire.<br /><br />3. Ironically, my old headset's issue was that I accidentally had the wire caught under my chair, and when I pulled the headset up from off my table, it broke a piece of plastic that allowed the cups to turn in those directions. However, after the onslaught of guerilla marketing she has unleashed upon the world (and I say that as a good thing), I decided to give it a try.  To say that I was pleasantly surprised would be an understatement.  This was an excellent story.<br /><br />I will spare you a synopsis of the book as the book's description handles it well.  While it only scratches the surface of what the book truly contains, I think to reveal anymore would do a disservice to those who have not read it.  It is a story that must be experienced to be believed.  Instead, I will hit on what I feel are the most important things that someone deciding to read this book should know.<br /><br />I have been reading Sci-Fi and Fantasy for many years and so one of the first things I look for is the world building.  Because I have read so much, I have a tendency to believe I have read it all.  However, I must say that Hamilton has surprised me with some very interesting concepts.  She has taken some different concepts and weaved them together into a world view that is pretty unique.  Her treatment of Wicca and the Salem witch trials proves she did at least a little research.  There were some other creatures of myth I recognized, so she must have done a little more research.  Having done a fair amount of research into many of these things myself, I really appreciated the effort.  Obviously this is a work of fiction, but I think some maybe surprised as to where the line between truth and fiction actually lies.  Ultimately, it is the way she blends vampires and shape shifters into these other concepts that is just brilliant and quite unique.  Like many, I suffer from vampire burnout but she manages to make it fresh and interesting again.  She has created a mythos in her world to serve as a backdrop for her characters that is a little bit like a lot ofthings but in the end is wholly different from them all.  For this reason alone, I enjoyed the book.<br /><br />The second thing I look at are the characters and hers are well written.  From the main characters to the supporting cast, all of them were realized enough to add to the story.  I found the main character of Sophia to be very authentic.  She reminded me of people that I have known and I found most of what she did to be quite realistic and wholly endearing.  The supporting characters were well done.  Not overdone nor underdone, they served the overall story.  As additional main characters were layered in, they blended into the story perfectly.  I thought the relationship between Sophia and Charles to be extremely well done.  At time I found some of their exchanges annoying but in a very real way.  If they bothered me at all, it was that they were sometimes too real.  It's sometimes hard to speak of realism when discussing fantasy novels but the overall realism of the characters is what really struck me.  The story and characters evolved naturally rather than just having the characters dive right into mortal peril.  Though it made the story advance more slowly, it made for a more convincing tale and I appreciate that.<br /><br />Finally, I look to the story itself and the writing.  Hamilton's writing style is very easy to read and connect with.  It is effective at engaging the reader and pulling them in to the story.  I was able to get into her style quickly which made it easier for her to grab my interest early.  The plot evolves at a nice pace and while it is impossible to not be able to predict some things, I can say there were a few surprises.  There is a flashback sequence that is a little lengthy for my taste given its location in the story, but it is full of important information so it was necessary.  The plot flowed nicely with its ups and downs.  There is high emotional content in many places including an ending that will cause some mixed feelings.Overall I was satisfied with the story.  Knowing this was going to be a series, I was pleased to see the story definitely ended.  Yes, it is also obvious it will continue but this part of the story was definitely resolved which was well done in my opinion.<br /><br />The only reason that I gave this book 4 stars is that in the end I was sort of right in the beginning, this isn't the type of story I would typically get into.  However, I can say that I was quite impressed with this book and when the next one comes out, I will read it.  If Rebecca can maintain this momentum, she will have a bright publishing future.  I, for one, can't wait to see it.

It's a tilt head, and doesn't have a swivel head like the more costly Nikon SB-700 offers.<br /><br />The angle of coverage on the SB-400 is 18mm (DX-format) and 27mm (FX-format).  For reference, this is the equivalent of about a 28mm lens on 35mm film camera.  The flash coverage is excellent and generally well distributed.  Tinting the head for a bounce effect will give you a softer effect, but if you need more, using a bounce card or the [[ASIN:B000PH1DL8 OM-400 Omni-Bounce Flash Diffuser for Nikon SB-400]] will work, and there are similar devices.<br /><br />Suggestion:<br /><br />For many, the SB-400 may be all that's needed as an excellent step upwards for Nikon DSLR owners who need more than their built-in flash offers, but some may be perhaps looking for more.  As stated earlier, this flash is the beginning, entry-level unit of the Nikon Creative Lighting System (CLS).  It supplements my aged Nikon SB-600 in that respect, but in truth I use the SB-400 far more on a day-in, day-out basis.  For those wishing to explore Nikon CLS and what can be done with it, I might suggest the [[ASIN:1118022238 Nikon Creative Lighting System Digital Field Guide]] by photographer Benjamin Edwards.  This well-written guide offers an excellent overview of the creative control of flash at the photographer's command, and with Nikon's Creative Lighting System, flash control can be almost instinctive, and the possibilities can be endless and limited to the users own creativity.  And there are enough creative tips, along with excellent full-color images and diagrams that can make use of the SB-400 even easier to understand.<br /><br />Summary:<br /><br />The Nikon SB-400 is an unpretentious, high-quality flash with all the features that many will actually use.  But do you actually need one of these, considering that the built-in flash on contemporary Nikon DSLRs (and the P7100) are so good?  Maybe not, but consider the SB-400 if you want an easy-to-use bounce flash, if you want faster recycle times than your camera may offer, and excellent coverage with your ultra-wide lenses.  You'll also get more shots per camera battery charge, since your camera's built-in flash uses 2-4 times as much power; using the SB-400 means you don't use the built-in flash. Other flash features can be gimmicks that may even interfere with taking pictures, and will slow you down rather than get the job done. But the film shows how wonderful it could sound via lossless once we get to the war.<br /><br />As one would expect from a film with war scenes, the film is absolutely immersive as you hear machine guns, tanks, cavalries and their horses marching and stomping to bomb blasts via LFE.  There is good use of the rear surround channels and panning through the surround channels and I was quite impressed by how the overall mix was.  \\"War Horse\\" has an amazing lossless soundtrack!<br /><br />Also, the musical soundtrack is wonderful.  The music is inspired by the serenity of the British landscapes and manages to capture that feel of adventure but also the horse as it goes through its adventures.  John Williams approach was to utilize brass instruments.<br /><br />The brass is the key to the music of \\"War Horse\\", creating emotion and feeling of the horse trough the ages.  Steven Spielberg said in the booklet notes of William's composition, \\"I feel that John has made a special gift to me of this music, which was inspired not only by my film but also by my many of the picturesque settings of the poet William Wordsworth, whose vivid descriptions of the British landscape inspired much of what you're going to hear.  I'm not sure what I can give John in return, other than a promise of more films to come...for as many more years as we both can imagine!\\".<br /><br />Subtitles are in English SDH, French and Spanish.<br /><br />SPECIAL FEATURES<br /><br />\\"War Horse\\" comes with the following special features:<br /><br />DISC 1:<br /><br />  War Horse: The Journey Home - (19:35) Featuring interviews with Steven Spielberg and the cast and a second roundtable interview with the creative team and crew.<br />  An Extra's Point of View - (3:06) A featurette about extra Martin D. All too often authors try to tell a good story with crap writing. It is also true that there are well written books that have drab and boring stories. Wool is awesome. The best of both worlds. B006JTS5OO|5.0|It all began back in 1954.<br /><br />A high school student named Jim Henson was working at WTOP-TV and creating puppets for a children's show known as \\"The Junior Morning Show\\".  Henson would continue making puppets as a Freshman at the University of Maryland, College Park as the teenager would go on to create the financially successful show \\"Sam and Friends\\" for WRC-TV.  The five minute show would feature a prototype of a character known as \\"Kermit the Frog\\".<br /><br />This would lead to Henson working on a public television children's shown known as \\"Sesame Street\\" in the late '60s where Kermit the Frog would appear as a news reporter.<br /><br />This would lead to Jim Henson creating a British/American television show known as \\"The Muppets\\" which premiered in 1976 and a total of five series were produced until March 1981 and lasting 120 episodes.  The show known for its parodies and slapstick would feature many celebrity guests and through the series, Jim Henson and Frank Oz would create additional characters to join Kermit the Frog such as Miss Piggy, Fozzie Bear, Scooter, Gonzo, the Swedish Chef, Rowlf the Dog, Dr. Bunsen Honeydew, Beaker, the band Dr. Teeth and the Electric Mayhem, Camilla the Chicken, Sam the Eagle, Statler and Waldorf, Link Hogthrob, Rizzo the Rat and many more.<br /><br />Although the television series ended, \\"The Muppets\\" would continue in the big screen with \\"The Muppet Movie\\" (1979), \\"The Great Muppet Caper\\" (1981), \\"The Muppets Take Manhattan\\" (1984), \\"The Muppet Christmas Carol\\" (1992), \\"Muppet Treasure Island\\" (1996) and \\"Muppets from Space\\" (1999).<br /><br />While a musical telefilm was created in 2005 titled \\"The Muppets' Wizard of Oz\\", the masses have not seen \\"The Muppets\\" for a long time and the last film was back in 1999.  Many have felt the characters have run its course and it was over, while others felt that without Jim Henson (who passed away in 1990), it would be difficult to continue \\"The Muppets\\" legacy.<br /><br />In 2008, actor Jason Segel (\\"How I Met Your Mother\\", \\"Forgetting Sarah Marshall\\") and writer/filmmaker Nicholas Stoller (\\"Fun with Dick and Jane\\", \\"Get Him to the Greek\\", \\"Yes Man\\") wanted to bring back the Muppets and so the two pitched the concept to Walt Disney and next thing you know, the film would be greenlighted and filmmaker James Bobin (\\"The Flight of the Conchords\\", \\"Da Ali G Show\\") would directed, while Jason Segel and Nicholas Stoller would be responsible for writing (Segel would also star in the film).<br /><br />Cast alongside Jason Segel would be Amy Adams (\\"The Fighter\\", \\"Night at the Museum: Battle of the Smithsonian\\", \\"Julie & Julia\\", \\"Enchanted\\") and Chris Cooper (\\"American Beauty\\", \\"The Bourne Identity\\", \\"Adaptation\\") as the antagonist.  The film would also feature talents such as Jack Black, Rashida Jones, Alan Arkin, Zach Galifianakis, Ken Jeong, Jim PArsons, Kristen Schaal, Eddie Pepitone, Sarah Silverman, Donald Glover, Emily Blunt, Leslie Feist, Whoopi Goldberg, Selena Gomez, Neil Patric Harris, Judd Hirsch, Kohn Krasinski, Mickey Rooney and more.<br /><br />The film would receive positive reviews from film critics and would be a success at the box office making over $158 million. For me, having grown up with the film, just to hear \\"Rainbow Connection\\" was quite sentimental as I have not heard Kermit the Frog sing that song since I was a child.<br /><br />Also, another positive that I found with this film is that it's a childrens film that doesn't have to utilize the latest in technology.<br /><br />In this day and age of CG graphics and technology, despite the characters being puppets, each character still retains their personality and watching them today, it still hasn't grown old to me. From brotherly relationships, dysfunctional relationships and of course, those who may have wondered what happened between Kermit the Frog and Miss Piggy is featured in this film.<br /><br />And while Jim Henson is no longer with us, nor is Frank Oz involved in voicing the characters any longer,  it does help that the voices today don't sound too different from the original voices. From there... things only get worse.<br /><br />I found \\"Catching Fire\\" to be a bit more sophisticated than the Hunger Games, and to some extent it can be read without having read \\"Hunger Games\\" (although it is not something that I would recommend). However, it certainly needs to be read with \\"Mockingjay\\" since the two books are completely dependent on each other. This may be biggest criticism, since these two books should have been combined into one, longer, novel.<br /><br /> I mentioned this in my review of \\"Hunger Games\\", but I think it deserved mentioning again, Collins writing has received some criticism for being too simplistic and having overly contrived dialogue. I think this is unfair. Collins writing may not approach Rowling's skills, but she is far from being a hack. What Collins may lack in terms of literary skill she make up for in pacing and the ability to pull the reader into a story that seems realistic. In this way she reminds a little of Jules Verne.<br /><br />Final Verdict - While I preferred \\"Hunger Games\\" I can say that \\"Catching Fire\\" is a worthy sequel that will leave very few fans disappointed.<br /><br />4 Stars

1451627289|5.0|Stephen King's newest novel, 11/22/63 was released in November of 2011 and debuted at number one on the New York Times Best Sellers list. It spent many weeks in the top 10 positions of this list and as of today it is being prepared as a paperback. This is the author's 50th novel and in my opinion one of his best works.<br /><br />For anyone who has never read a Stephen King book this would be a great book to start with. As with many of his novels, Stephen King uses this book to take a normal citizen, in this case a teacher, and throw them into unusual circumstances. The author captures the naivety of the character through his amazing gift of prose. While reading this book I was able to feel as though I was the protagonist of the story.<br /><br />Not only was this book a great example of Stephen King's fictional writing abilities but it was also the first time I have ever seen him take on historical facta in such depth. Being as a lot of the story takes place in the past, the author must have done a tremendous amount of research in order to capture that setting. Intertwined with the main story arc were many side stories that captured the feelings and emotions of the people of that era. For anyone that is interested in the history of the 1950s and 1960s and also likes a good story this book is a must read.<br /><br />As with many of Stephen King's books this one is packed with a lot of suspense. There are so many twists and turns that you are never sure if the protagonist will complete the task that he set out to accomplish. These twists are what make this book a compelling read for people of all ages. I would recommend this book to anyone that wants to be engrossed in a very satisfying story.

B007J4T2G8|5.0|First, I am not sure why I'm even writing a review on a book that has over 2k opinions. Second, I really should rate Fifty Shades with 3 stars.<br /><br />As other reviewers have pointed out, Fifty Shades is in need of an editing pencil. And, yes, James is shockingly repetitive in her use of actions, non sequiters, and dialogue. She tries too hard to get her point across with repetitions of action and thought that the reader really gets -- and no reader wants to think that the writer believes them to be stupid. Take the beginning, when protagonist Ana Steele constantly refers to the antagonist, Christian Grey has a \\"control freak.\\" We got it the first time, Ms. James ... we especially get it because we know going in that Grey is a BSMD kind of lover. Each of these can be irritating, but they're also easily forgiven. You should already know that the strength of the book is hardly because it's highbrow fiction.<br /><br />Fifty Shades is a compulsive read. No Tess of the D'Urbervilles to be sure (which is definitely NOT a compulsive read), but the novel is is fast-paced and easy to read. I flipped the pages quickly in anticipation of what would come next. (Um, no ... that would not be for the naughty bits, but the progression of the story and character development. The naughty bits, I must say, appear too often and aren't particularly interesting ... it's really kind of like reading a the letters in a well-known \\"gentleman') magazine. I began to ho-hum after the 3rd scene. There really are better devices to acknowledge a continuing passionate sexual relationship, and it doesn't have to be detailing each intimate moment. But this is James's weakness -- repetition, ad nauseum. Repeating word for word the \\"agreement\\" -- 3 times I think, was also a bit of overkill.)<br /><br />I *cared* about these characters. I was especially drawn in by Christian Grey ... why there are 50 shades of him. He is one of those enigmatic men: tender and kind, demanding, controlling, sweet, and totally messed up. I'm curious as to what makes him tick, and I hope that when I read the subsequent books, it's worth my time. Ana's own struggles to understand him are compelling, but in this, too, the stream-of-thought storytelling -- which isn't bad -- tells far too much than the narrative has already shown. Fifty Shades of Grade is strongly about the sexual awakening of a young woman and it does a decent job of working through it.<br /><br />Part of the book is written via emails between Ana and Christian. I found this to be clever and enjoyed what they would say to each other. While I would hardly call Fifty Shades funny or humorous, there is enough within the email and repartee between characters to bring on a smile or even a chuckle.<br /><br />Why the 5 stars, which I usually reserve for well-written literary fiction? Because, quite simply, Fifty Shades is like candy ... yummy, irresistible, and a complete escape from reality. While stylistically cumbersome, it's quite good enough.

1423113381|4.0|***NOTE MY REVIEWS OFTEN CONTAIN SPOILERS***<br /><br />When Carter Kane arrives with his father to visit his sister Sadie in London, he expects it to be like any of the other one day visitations he has with his sister twice a year.  He doesn't expect to find himself at the British Museum watching his father blow up the Rosetta stone using magic Carter had never known existed.  However that's what occurs and it leaves Sadie and Carter with no one left but each other.  Deported from England they arrive in New York at the House of Life's 21st Nome in New York City and begin a journey neither of them could have ever imagine.  Set, an Egyptian god that neither believed before was real wants to kill them.  The House of Life thinks they're godlings and wants them dead as well and their new guardian their Uncle Amos disappears.  Are they the unwitting hosts of gods?  And will they manage to stay alive long enough to discover what really happened to their Dad and while they're at it save the world before Set turns the earth into a barren wasteland?<br /><br />I gave this book less than a perfect rating for one reason and one reason alone.  It wasn't the way it was written because in fact Riordan has presented a thrilling page turner from beginning to end in The Red Pyramid.  My issue with this book is that too many readers in the target audience will miss out on a great story because they're daunted by the massive length of the novel.  All three of my children are middle grade readers and I've been trying to convince them to read this since right after  I read this book the first time, which was within the first week it was released.  Even my oldest who actually really enjoys reading won't touch this book because she looks at the size of it then looks at me like I'm insane.  Her student dictionary is not as thick as The Red Pyramid and she doesn't enjoy reading that.  Kennedy, my daughter, is quick to devour books that are around half the length of this one which for an eleven year old fairly respectable, but she looks at this book and sees its size and concludes that it must be boring because only text books and dictionary compare in length.  Obviously many adult novels also compare but I'm quite certain my children believe that adults enjoy reading the dictionary and don't mind boring books.  She considers that a part of growing up, becoming boring.  And of course she's not ready to be boring yet, so no matter what I say she won't even attempt to read this book.  It's disappointing because all three of my children are missing out on a truly epic story.  So I can't in good faith give this novel a perfect rating because it doesn't matter how great the story is if the target audience is afraid to read it, then the journey isn't traveled and it's kind of like that tree falls in the forest idea.  Does it make a sound?  Well in some ways I say no because how is there sound if no one can hear it?  If a second edition of the novel were released in two or even better three parts making them an appealing length to average middle grade readers I would say the book deserved a perfect rating because then the target audience would be enticed to read it.  Riordan may write middle grade fiction at the moment, but I'd venture to guess that probably more than half of his readership, even of his middle grade fiction, is adult readers.  Adults aren't daunted by a long novel if it promises a good story, most children are.  So what I'm saying is I think the publisher should consider releasing a serialized version of the first novel in the Kane Chronicles so that the target audience won't be afraid to take the journey.<br /><br />The plot of this novel is a page turner from beginning to end and features an interesting point of view.  It's a first person novel, but it alternates between Carter and Sadie's points of view and separates them by chapter, labeling each chapter with the narrator so as you're clear on who is telling you the story. What's even more interesting is that the story is sandwiched between author letters than kind of set the personality of the story. In his letters, which are just as much fiction as the story itself Riordan claims to have received an audio recording of the story within and does not know whether the story enclosed is fiction or fact.  It seems to unreal to be fact, but the amount of factual detail included means that either the narrators are incredible well researched or their outrageous tale is true.  I was hooked on this story at the beginning of the letter from the author.  It reminded me a lot of how Elizabeth Kostova's Historian opened, sending chills down the spine as you look over your shoulder for the mysterious danger that comes of reading the tale.  After setting a wonderful feel for the novel you begin reading the double first person narrative which is filled with rich and vivid description and a roller coaster fast pace that leaves you devouring the pages from beginning to end.  In addition to offering an excellent story, the book is filled with educational information about Egyptian mythology and history.  The main plot is an adventure tale steeped in mythology and filled with magic, but the book also features a couple of subplots in which Carter and Sadie learn to be what they haven't been since Sadie was six, a real family.  They learn how to deal with the changes in their lives, the pain of the past and each other.  The book also features a semi love story for both the hero and heroine though neither of these are dramatically played up it's enough to snag interest and make you wonder if there's more to come for the siblings.  This is one of those stories that I'd deem a must read, don't miss it tale.<br /><br />Riordan as always has created impressive and intriguing characters.  He begins with the hero and heroine, siblings Carter and Sadie who come from a mixed race family where each of them favors the opposite parent so they don't actually look related.  So in that alone the characters are intriguing because they deal with multiple prejudices which only a person of mixed race could really understand in the way they can.  And we can see how this affects who they've become as we get to know them.  Carter's Dad has drilled into him that he has to always be impeccable if he wants to be treated with any respect, he doesn't dress like an average teen because when he isn't well dressed people look at him as if he's a criminal because he's of African American descent.  Every time anyone asks Sadie about her family she somehow becomes an outsider or outcast, she's always different, never normal, so she gave up trying to fit in and streaks her hair with crayola colors, wearing combat boots with even her school uniform.  Carter has always felt like since he's the child with his father, a man who's lost not only his wife, but his daughter, he has to do his best to make his dad happy.  He follows orders, listens to directions and you see how he bites his tongue and does his best to be accommodating because of the hand life has dealt him.  Sadie feel resentful of being left to be raised by her grandparents instead of staying with her father so she's outspoken and rebellious.  Each of them is envious of the other's life but neither of them understands what that life was really like, nor do they at the beginning understand each other.  Even without the whole magic and Egyptian god twist these would be interesting characters.  But when you add that to the mix the characters become more, the kind of characters that stick with you and seem larger than life.  Carter and Sadie display excellent emotional depth and are very easy to relate to feel for and root for.  What's more both leading character show an extreme amount of character growth throughout the course of the novel.  It's not an instantaneous thing.  You can see with each scene and each experience how it is both shaping them and changing them. Additionally the secondary character presented in this novel are at the very least distinctive and for the most part well developed.  No character introduced here lays flat on the page, they each come alive in a way that goes beyond your average book and shows Riordan's rare talent for character creation.<br /><br />Overall I would definitely recommend this book to pretty much everyone.  Again the only thing I can think to note in a negative manner is that the length is daunting to many middle grade readers, but those willing to take on a book so lengthy will be rewarded with a journey unlike any other.  Highly recommended.alone the characters are intriguing because they deal with multiple prejudices which only a person of mixed race could really understand in the way they can. What's more both leading character show an extreme amount of character growth throughout the course of the novel.  It's not an instantaneous thing.  You can see with each scene and each experience how it is both shaping them and changing them. Additionally the secondary character presented in this novel are at the very least distinctive and for the most part well developed.  No character introduced here lays flat on the page, they each come alive in a way that goes beyond your average book and shows Riordan's rare talent for character creation.<br /><br />Overall I would definitely recommend this book to pretty much everyone.  Again the only thing I can think to note in a negative manner is that the length is daunting to many middle grade readers, but those willing to take on a book so lengthy will be rewarded with a journey unlike any other.  Highly recommended.

030746363X|4.0|There are a lot of interesting concepts in the book, but the most important one is that personal experimentation with before and after measurement will tell you what works for you. Some of the negative reviews are making blanket complaints either about particular statements (starch metabolism, protein effects, etc.) or blanket condemnations that some of it just couldn't possibly work (nothing is this fast, particular routines didn't work for the reviewer, etc.)<br /><br />This misses the point, people. Ferriss is recounting his experiences, and suggesting that they may work for you as well, but his main suggestion is for you to try and evaluate things for yourself. I don't see any way to prove that Ferriss is lying about his personal results, fantastic though they seem to be, but absent a third party lab test I also have no reason to say he's not telling the truth. Bodies are notoriously variable in their response to exercise and diet, so even if every one of Ferriss's experiments is true as expressed, the sample size of one doesn't offer a lot of confidence that they will have the same effect on you. However, for the experimentally inclined, this book provides a lot of starting points for trying to shortcut the traditional body building grind en route to various strength and appearance targets.<br /><br />This is a fairly quick read, and I personally found the ideas interesting enough to try a few out (with results at least as good as any other effective exercise book). Hyperbolic subtitle notwithstanding, I'm glad I read it and that Ferriss seems to be willing to do and recount extreme experiments on himself for the rest of us to consider without going first.

B003MC5N28|4.0|I wonder why utopias are so frightening.<br /><br />Jonas has a childhood many people would wish for. He has structure, order, purpose, affection. He is never hungry or frightened. He knows his place in the world, and he is content with it. Then, as he turns 12, he is selected for a special job, one that destroys his world by making him look deeper into it, to understand why it happens the way it does, what the moral cost of his comfort is.<br /><br />I should have read this book years ago. My mom finally gave up and bought it for my Kindle because we were talking about THE TERRORISTS OF IRUSTAN, and bounded societies.<br /><br />The Sameness reminded me of the dys/utopia in Madeline L'Engle's A WRINKLE IN TIME, where all the children bounce balls at the same time, and all the mothers call the children in at the same time. There is obviously this thread of fear and horror in both books about being required to be regimented, or pay a terrible price. I wonder if it's a coincidence that THE GIVER and WRINKLE IN TIME were written by women  at a time when women were fighting to get out of the regimented and nicey-nicey world. To go on one's own, beyond the boundaries of civilization and rules, is fearful and dangerous and you don't know how it's going to end, but sometimes you have to head for the woods, or the giant throbbing brain, whichever is relevant. Interestingly, Lowry's character, Jonas, is male, and I would perhaps have found the story less interesting if he were female, because the choice he made might be considered traditionally feminine. The gender roles were actually pretty interesting through the whole book.<br /><br />I thought the characters were sketched in lightly, but the plot and problem were compelling, and it almost makes sense that personality was not something Jonas thought a lot about. Lowry did a great job of capturing the incuriousity some children have about adult things.<br /><br />Read if: You want to think about what you are used to and the value or lack thereof in compliance and equality.<br /><br />Skip if: You are horrified by utopias, your issues are not about fear of being the same.e value or lack thereof in compliance and equality.<br /><br />Skip if: You are horrified by utopias, your issues are not about fear of being the same.

B0080KHWJY|5.0|While similar in many respects to Pillars of the Earth, including most of the same flaws (though usually to a lesser degree), World Without End is a much more mature work.  It is simultaneously more Romantic and less romanticized, in the best and worst sense of the terms, respectively.  The plot is somewhat tighter, the characterization is a bit deeper, and it's all integrated around a central theme: independence, in all the major aspects of life---intellectual, economic, and romantic.<br /><br />The story centers around the love of a man who wants to build the tallest structure in fourteenth-century England and a woman devoted to medicine.  The latter storyline is especially compelling, as she tries to cope with outbreaks of the plague as well as she can, without simply acceding to the authority of the Ancients (such as Hippocrates).  She follows her own mind, even when that brings her into conflict with the Church and gives rise to accusations of heresy and witchcraft.  Follett does a much better job here than he did in Pillars of the Earth showing, in keeping with his theme, that religious authoritarianism is fundamentally opposed to the independent mind.<br /><br />(Unfortunately, Follett undercuts his theme of independence somewhat with the explicit suggestion toward the end that moral character is inherited from one's ancestry just like, say, the color of one's hair.  But that idea is somewhat, though not entirely, mitigated by the actual events of the story, and in any case is a minor flaw in what is by and large an excellent piece of work.)<br /><br />There is a lot else going on in this epic story as well, and the whole thing is a very good read.  Four and a half stars.

0307956350|3.0|In the spirit of the atypical James Bond novel, [[ASIN:0141045051 The Spy Who Loved Me]], this debut effort is as much about the dynamics of romantic relationships as it is about secret agents. Chris Pavone's The Expats also offers realistic descriptions of European domestic life--the author lived in Luxembourg. Better than another recent spy thriller, [[ASIN:0670023213 The Spy Who Jumped Off the Screen]], The Expats finds similar inspiration from the genre's leading men, in particular, [[ASIN:B000W07EKW Jason Bourne]]. There's a covert meeting in Berlin (\\"Same time tomorrow, north end of Kollwitzplatz, Prenzlauerberg.\\") plus another scene on the Pont Neuf, just like in The Bourne Identity. There's even an assassination gone wrong, complicated by an unexpected family member, just like in The Bourne Supremacy.<br /><br />Pavone offers some nice descriptive turns: \\"Snowflakes began to flutter, drifting softly to the street lined with eighteenth-century &lt;&lt;hotels particuliers>>, massive arched doors leading to courtyards, a matching suite of three imposing buildings leaning against one another, like fashion models posing for a homoerotic shot, skin-on-skin-on-skin.\\" (p.174) Sometimes, however, he can't help but go overboard on the similes: \\"A train traversed the gorge on the high aqueduct-style bridge. On the semi-frozen river below, a duck quacked insistently, sounding like a grumpy old man arguing with a cashier.\\" (p.296)<br /><br />My main gripe is that the action jumps around too much, both across time and scene settings. Furthermore, the final three chapters resort far too much on exposition. And a few period pop culture references will not age well: \\"Like Travolta, near-dancing in Pulp Fiction.\\" And yeah, maybe it's not believable that a wife could keep her fifteen-year CIA career a secret from her husband. Overall, however, this was an enjoyable read.<br /><br />3.5 stars<br /><br />[The reviewer was provided with a complimentary copy of the book.]

1591844096|3.0|I agree with most of what Godin says, although not for reasons he makes clear.  To begin with he seems to be arguing that it todays economy if you are not a creative problem solver then you will not likely prosper. This is good advice; when a $ 500 nail gun can drive 43000 nails per hour, no one needs run of the mill hammer swingers on a construction site anymore, and computers have created a similar situation in many white collar jobs. But Godin is urging us to be more than just creative problem solvers in the workplace. He wants us all to be true to our personal artistic urges and finally use our humanity to make a difference in the lives of others.  When we do this we become a lynchpin and life will be great. The subtext is that we can all become Steve Jobses or Bill Gateses.  However, I see three diferent types of lynchpins in the book each with different rewards. The creative problem solver in the workplace is the first type and it is definately usefull for career sucsess these days. However, probably many such creative problem sovlers also helped design Auchwitz. The second type of lynchpin is true to his art. This is just as likely to bring poverty and obscurity as it is to bring fame and fortune, and many great artists and philosophers were never recongized in thier lifetimes. And the third type of lynchpin is true to thier humanity, and this can often lead to death. For every Nelson Mandella, Vaclav Havel or Tuva Belski, thousands or millions of brave and noble soals perished in obscurity.  I believe it is worthwhile stiving to by a lynchpin in all the senses described by Godin and have often strived to do so. But to expect that being a lynchpin in the second two senses will bring fame and fortune is to court bitterness and disapointment in life. Indeed there are times when life calls on us to be a moral lynchpin, such as when careing for an elderly relative, helping a child through a learning disability or saving a broken family from collapse, that will never bring us any tangible reward, and sometimes the people who benefit most from these instances will never even know what we did for them.  Such efforts can even draw us away from the more glamourous artistic and career pursuits often described in Godin's examples of the lynchpin life. These efforts however are still worth it, simply not for reasons Godin makes clear.  If Godin wants to become a moral philosopher, and with his tallent I am sure his contributions would be welcome,  he needs to put more emotional work into his art.
